  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_216c8224-a1f8-4c46-a72c-0703f34d9d3c'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<i>Let them swim to the bottom of the ocean.</i> -Don Domenico Clericuzio</blockquote><br/><br/>...and this badass Don who has ran a crime family for decades actually means what he said, guys.<br/><br/>...and this, is my most favorite line from this book. <br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1515252497ra/24846619.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>(Link: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://giphy.com/gifs/movie-godfather-the-3o7bujq5yoFwhPClKE'>https://giphy.com/gifs/movie-godfathe...</a>)<br/><br/>Strange that I was clueless about what Don Clericuzio actually meant when he causally commented (usually in the middle of his business meetings wi</span>
  <span id="freeText4683966788211250790" style="display:none">Review for the Chinese translation of this book: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3556267439">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br><br><blockquote>
<i>Let them swim to the bottom of the ocean.</i> -Don Domenico Clericuzio</blockquote><br><br>...and this badass Don who has ran a crime family for decades actually means what he said, guys.<br><br>...and this, is my most favorite line from this book. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1515252497ra/24846619.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>(Link: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://giphy.com/gifs/movie-godfather-the-3o7bujq5yoFwhPClKE">https://giphy.com/gifs/movie-godfathe...</a>)<br><br>Strange that I was clueless about what Don Clericuzio actually meant when he causally commented (usually in the middle of his business meetings with his sons and trusted nephew, etc) 'let them/him/her swim to the bottom of the ocean'; the true meaning of these words had totally escaped me, but once I realized its true meaning, I was thrilled!<br><br>Okay, back to the story itself. At the beginning of the book, we see Don Clericuzio ( who is at the height of his power) made plan for his loved ones: the Family would withdraw itself (mostly) from illegal dealings, his three sons would go into different legal (but money-making) businesses; as to his trusted nephew Joseph 'Pippi' Le Dena, while the man would continue his job as the Family's number one hitman, still the Don arranged for him to move to Las Vegas to look after the Family's gamble business.<br><br>Pippi was one of the few people whom the Don trusted the most, but he had done one thing which weakened his position within the Family: he had married a beautiful wife who could hardly fit into the Family, even worse, the poor woman had no idea who her husband really was.<br><br>With the Don's blessing, Pippi's newborn son Cross, and the Don's own grandson Dante (son of the Don's widowed daughter) were christened at the very same day. The Don wished for these two infants to grow up and one day move on to the 'society', yet he could not foresee the fiery rivalry between Cross and Dante after they grew up, and the conflicts within the Family could easily disturb the future of the Clericuzio clan.<br><br>The Clericuzio clan is...you guessed it....is quite a copy of the Corleone Family from <i>The Godfather</i>: one badass Don as the family's leader, three sons, one widowed daughter, one adopted son/nephew, a wife who only slowly comes to realize her husband's secrets, blah blah blah. The similarities in the character's setting and the story's structure are undeniable. <br><br>However, in <i>The Last Don</i> we see a lot more about how the members of the third generation of a powerful crime family would turn up to be: we get the story of Cross and Dante, as they represent two different future for the Family.<br><br>As a main character, Cross has better development and internal struggles than Dante--the latter is painted as a violent, unlikable jerk throughout the book. We see the 20 years old Cross entered the 'family's business' as a young adult, slowly building up his reputation through the years, facing difficult missions and setbacks, and struggling between loyalty for his father (Pippi) and/or the Family and his goodwill. <br><br>I also like how we can see how a crime family operates, how these people run their business and how they 'deal' with traitors and enemies (all written in a down-to-earth, matter-of-fact manner) through the storylines of Cross and his father.<br><br>PS: Pippi's story really reminds me of a Chinese slang: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<i>a hunting hound always winds up dying in the wilderness</i> (獵狗終須山上喪) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>This time I'm glad to see in this story, women actually act as their own free agents instead of just voiceless, obedient wives, mothers and daughters like they did in The Godfather! Therefore female characters such as Claudia (Cross's younger sister) and Athena (well...the love interest) having their own storylines is quite refreshing (a lesbian director also plays a small part).<br><br>However! I really don't like all those plot lines and in-jokes about the showbiz in Hollywood and how people run business in the La la Land! <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1515252497ra/24846621.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>(Link: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://giphy.com/gifs/movie-godfather-the-l4FGA5yLpEhfONOtW">https://giphy.com/gifs/movie-godfathe...</a>)<br><br>I know, Mario Puzo had been a script writer for Hollywood for years therefore he naturally knew a lot of insider's information; and he didn't shy away from making jokes about how script-writers and novelists were treated like third-class citizens in the showbiz. But! As light-hearted and delightful those Hollywood characters can be, all these lengthy Hollywood-plots still weights the whole story down!!!!<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1515252497ra/24846620.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>(Link: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://giphy.com/gifs/movie-godfather-the-3o7bu5qlJXEXDK4gWk">https://giphy.com/gifs/movie-godfathe...</a>)<br><br>Yet, the ending part really does make up for the shortcoming of this novel.<br><br>Did I mention that this book isn't quite up-there with The Godfather? I mean.........nothing beats The Godfather, anyway. XD<br><br>I think the main reason why The Last Don fails to outperform The Godfather is that the sense of urgency in the story and my own emotional investment with the characters is both weaker this time. In The Godfather, the Corleone Family is at great risk when we meet them: Don Vito Corleone was injured and his son Mike must recuse his father and siblings, who wouldn't be touched by the loyalty and sadness he displays?<br><br>In the case of the Clericuzio Family, they are already secured in their power when we meet them and there isn't much of a crisis to be spoken of (until the ending part, that is). As to the romance(s)...let's face it, despite Mr. Puzo's mastery at crafting his world of outlaws and organized crime, he just wasn't as great when he was writing about the romantic relationships between men and women.<br><br>Review for The Godfather: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1363548268?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="4683966788211250790" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer17350077930473394185">Jar of Death Pick #37<br/><br/>4.5 Stars! <br/><br/>I discovered The Last Don before I had ever even heard of The Godfather. When I was a kid I watched The Last Don on I believe CBS as a Movie of the Week(fellow old people do you remember those?) and I really liked it. I told my mom about it and she mentioned The Godfather, she said I should watch it, so we went to Blockbuster ( God! I'm old) and I rented all 3 Godfather movies. I obviously loved the first two and I no longer acknowledge Part III. I then went to Bo</span>
  <span id="freeText17350077930473394185" style="display:none">Jar of Death Pick #37<br /><br />4.5 Stars! <br /><br />I discovered The Last Don before I had ever even heard of The Godfather. When I was a kid I watched The Last Don on I believe CBS as a Movie of the Week(fellow old people do you remember those?) and I really liked it. I told my mom about it and she mentioned The Godfather, she said I should watch it, so we went to Blockbuster ( God! I'm old) and I rented all 3 Godfather movies. I obviously loved the first two and I no longer acknowledge Part III. I then went to Borders(I should live in a nursing home) and bought The Godfather and The Last Don in book form. <br /><br />The Godfather I read and liked at the time but as I've aged I've grown to love it more and more. The Last Don novel I didn't like at all. I thought it was boring and until now I had never picked it up again. So imagine my surprise when on a second reading of this I loved this book! It is a lot slower paced than The Godfather but I could appreciate that more as an adult. <br /><br />The Last Don is about.... The Mafia and Hollywood and Las Vegas. Instead of following the Corleone family this time we follow the Clericuzio family. While there were obvious parallels between the two I felt like they were two completely different families. The Last Don despite the title is less about The Don and more about the younger less criminally inclined generation. I liked the push and pull between the criminal side and the legal side. The Last Don is less violent then The Godfather but its still a brutal read. <br /><br />I don't need to recommend a Mario Puzo novel because either you're into Mafia novels or you're not. But I loved it!</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3491681695" />
    <a title="Azet" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42316620-azet"><img alt="Azet" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546348551p2/42316620.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3491681695?book_show_action=true">Aug 28, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Azet" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Azet" href="/user/show/42316620-azet">Azet</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099533245" href="/book/show/6690794-the-last-don">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42316620-azet?shelf=books-made-into-movies">books-made-into-movies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42316620-azet?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42316620-azet?shelf=drama-family-life">drama-family-life</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3491681695" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11966786547974781033">I have now finished reading the swedish translation of Mario Puzo`s "The Last Don" which is another crime mystery thriller that takes you into the powerful empire of the Clericuzios.Like many other readers,i couldn`t help but compare this novel to the classic favourite "The Godfather".Puzo have a way of writing i have never experienced before in other novels.He writes in a way that gives me goosebumps and knows how to draw you into his world and make me care about his characters,good or evil.Dom</span>
  <span id="freeText11966786547974781033" style="display:none">I have now finished reading the swedish translation of Mario Puzo`s "The Last Don" which is another crime mystery thriller that takes you into the powerful empire of the Clericuzios.Like many other readers,i couldn`t help but compare this novel to the classic favourite "The Godfather".Puzo have a way of writing i have never experienced before in other novels.He writes in a way that gives me goosebumps and knows how to draw you into his world and make me care about his characters,good or evil.Domenico Clericuzio is the Don in this tale of destruction and betrayal and is obsessed with the dream of making his family business legal one day in the future for the sake of his grandchildren.But the past war against the other Mafia clan Santiados has left a terrible secret that threatens to destroy everything he holds dear.<br /><br />I loved reading this novel,especially the parts with the Clericuzios who intrigued me with their Anti-hero characteristics but had great honor and loyalty towards each other.Their clans greatest assasin was Don`s nephew Joseph "Pippi" De Lena who was a mix in between Michael and Sonny Corleone which made him a very strong character in my eyes.His son who is actually the main-character in this novel Cross De Lena was another favourite character of mine since he represented much more humanity than the other Clericuzios since he often questioned their morals in life.I feel kinda bad for saying this,but i disliked all the female characters in this story despite their feminist view of the world and were women who had backbones in front of the Clericuzio clan.To me they were never ahead in front of the Clericuzio men,but garn damn how they sounded stupid every time they tried to outflank them.One scene for an example is when Pippi`s wife Nalene refused to share the custody of their son and daughter when they agreed to divorce.That scene made me want to slap her,especially since she did it with such great disrespect when she knew what a dangerous man Pippi actually was if he was to become your enemy.I also disliked Cross love-interest,the actress Athena.She was made for the readers to sympathize with her,but i found her so incredibly boring and stupid.For once i wished for Cross to not end up with her boring ass.Then we have Cross sister Claudia Clericuzio who becomes a script writer in Hollywood-gosh every scene with her made me cringe!Then we have the lifestyle of Hollywood and their disgusting non-morals and hypocrisy.That`s a world i want to have no part of since the famous people there can`t ever figure out how to be happy.<br /><br />I do not regret reading "The Last Don",its a work of art and i loved following the Clericuzio clan.Don Clericuzio is a complex but very ruthless Don,who is more feared than loved.He allowed no mistakes in operations or plans but still had a forgiving streak when it came to his family.He tried to give his growing psychopath of a grandchild a chance while growing up,but that one betrayal destroyed every chances.I did not expect that Don had seen so far ahead of the consequences of that one betrayal,and my respect for him only strengthened after that.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11966786547974781033" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer574444846342852114">Mario Puzo's <i>The Last Don</i> translation in simplified Chinese......well to tell the truth the translation itself is...........messy, the translator clearly didn't get many of the English/American slang and some of the words simply got translated WRONGLY, so it's very off putting. <br/><br/>Plus, to make matter worse, there is one rape scene in the novel and due to the shitty books censorship in mainland China (which I hate), this scene got cut out in a really clumsy manner, readers most likely won't know wh</span>
  <span id="freeText574444846342852114" style="display:none">Mario Puzo's <i>The Last Don</i> translation in simplified Chinese......well to tell the truth the translation itself is...........messy, the translator clearly didn't get many of the English/American slang and some of the words simply got translated WRONGLY, so it's very off putting. <br /><br />Plus, to make matter worse, there is one rape scene in the novel and due to the shitty books censorship in mainland China (which I hate), this scene got cut out in a really clumsy manner, readers most likely won't know what is going on in this scene because the whole paragraphs got cut up so badly. That's so insulting to the readers, it feels like the fools within the Chinese government believes readers cannot handle reading about one rape scene when there has already been people getting murdered, their corpses gotten sank into the sea and all this violence shit in the story.<br /><br />*sighs* At least I borrowed this book from the library and reading in Chinese took a lot less effort and energy than the original English version.<br /><br />Last but not least, although I'm glad in this novel women are treated as people who rightfully have their own desires, careers and stuff but it is still icky when Mr. Puzo talked about Hollywood actresses have to use sex in exchange for leading roles and stuff. Completely icky.</span>

<div id="review_3600189531" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3600189531" />
    <a title="Forthbridge" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16616688-forthbridge"><img alt="Forthbridge" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3600189531?book_show_action=true">Oct 17, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Forthbridge" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Forthbridge" href="/user/show/16616688-forthbridge">Forthbridge</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3600189531" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2453866577025937013">I got this from Audible on a Sale item and glad I did. I was expecting a fairly lightweight 'boy book' which would be a distraction from Covid/Brexit and Trump. It was far more than that, giving a lucid explanation of the Sicilian system of warlords offering governance in return for tribal loyalty and economic control. <br/>The description of Mob methods sounded completely authentic and tied in with Norman Lewis' account of post war Italian history. <br/>Mario Puzo certainly knew how to plot and pace a no</span>
  <span id="freeText2453866577025937013" style="display:none">I got this from Audible on a Sale item and glad I did. I was expecting a fairly lightweight 'boy book' which would be a distraction from Covid/Brexit and Trump. It was far more than that, giving a lucid explanation of the Sicilian system of warlords offering governance in return for tribal loyalty and economic control. <br />The description of Mob methods sounded completely authentic and tied in with Norman Lewis' account of post war Italian history. <br />Mario Puzo certainly knew how to plot and pace a novel. He made the serious point that many mobsters, like everyone else, would like a settled family life but their way of earning a crust makes that highly unlikely.<br />The 'might is right' and disregard for civilised norms themes in the novel reminded me of Trump and his admiration for hard men like Putin, so my efforts at escapism were not entirely successful.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2453866577025937013" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4635714342572368393">Similar to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22034.The_Godfather' title='The Godfather by Mario Puzo' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Godfather</a> in many ways, it didn't seem to be as tight of a story, but it's been years since I last read it, so I can't say for certain. It wasn't bad, but I didn't like any of the characters as much nor was there as much violence. All in all, it was a west coast version mostly set few decades later, an imitation.<br/><br/>I did get a kick out of the writer character.   <span id="freeText4635714342572368393" style="display:none">Similar to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22034.The_Godfather" title="The Godfather by Mario Puzo" rel="nofollow noopener">The Godfather</a> in many ways, it didn't seem to be as tight of a story, but it's been years since I last read it, so I can't say for certain. It wasn't bad, but I didn't like any of the characters as much nor was there as much violence. All in all, it was a west coast version mostly set few decades later, an imitation.<br /><br />I did get a kick out of the writer character. Quite a bit of time is spent on Hollywood studios screwing everyone on contracts, especially the writers. Net points are dreams of money &amp; never pay out due to creative accounting, as this writer finds out the hard way. Then the studio goes on to make millions on sequels &amp; doesn't have to pay the author because they bought the characters, too. I wonder how much autobiographical info is in this story?<br /><br />Not bad, but I'm glad I just borrowed the audio book from the library &amp; didn't pay for it.</span>

<div id="review_395905470" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/395905470" />
    <a title="emily cryptic" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1859704-emily-cryptic"><img alt="emily cryptic" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1346940128p2/1859704.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/395905470?book_show_action=true">Aug 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="emily cryptic" class="user" itemprop="url" name="emily cryptic" href="/user/show/1859704-emily-cryptic">emily cryptic</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer395905470" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1407776216462198179">Like most of the others who have reviewed this book, I thought it was much too long. My favorite review so far was simply "too many chapters." But Puzo's problem in this book extends beyond excessive chapters, or length; it's that he tried to write two different stories in one, with too many characters and too little tension. The story about the Clericuzio family is actually quite interesting and well crafted, but Puzo drags out the set-up for 300 pages, finally puts together some genuine plot m</span>
  <span id="freeText1407776216462198179" style="display:none">Like most of the others who have reviewed this book, I thought it was much too long. My favorite review so far was simply "too many chapters." But Puzo's problem in this book extends beyond excessive chapters, or length; it's that he tried to write two different stories in one, with too many characters and too little tension. The story about the Clericuzio family is actually quite interesting and well crafted, but Puzo drags out the set-up for 300 pages, finally puts together some genuine plot movement, then rushes the ending, at just under 500 pages, making both the journey and the payoff frustrating and unsatisfying. I was thrilled to finally be done with the book, but I also put it down yearning for more of the good stuff. Many of the Hollywood characters are so forgettable, I struggled to understand why they were a part of the story at all. Cross, Pippi and The Don are far and away the most compelling element of the story. I would have skipped the first 300 pages of movie industry garbage and gotten right to the heart of the Clericuzio struggles. I loved The Godfather, and I do love Puzo's storytelling--he is a very talented writer. But he needed to pick between indulging in his bitter Hollywood rant, or nailing another intriguing mob thriller. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1407776216462198179" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_342915044" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/342915044" />
    <a title="Luka Novak" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6752257-luka-novak"><img alt="Luka Novak" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325253543p2/6752257.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/342915044?book_show_action=true">Jun 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Luka Novak" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Luka Novak" href="/user/show/6752257-luka-novak">Luka Novak</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6752257-luka-novak?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer342915044" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17411045268450190413">Weak and long-winded. I understand that ever Puzo's book will be compared to Godfather and judged by its standard, but this book is weak even without that comaprisson. <br/><br/>The premise of the book is that Don Clericuzio wants to retire from mafia business and "go legit" and how he does that. In book's opening he announces this intention to the family, wishing that his children, and nephew will start legitimate businesses and their children reap the benefits.<br/><br/>So far so good but then the story splits in</span>
  <span id="freeText17411045268450190413" style="display:none">Weak and long-winded. I understand that ever Puzo's book will be compared to Godfather and judged by its standard, but this book is weak even without that comaprisson. <br /><br />The premise of the book is that Don Clericuzio wants to retire from mafia business and "go legit" and how he does that. In book's opening he announces this intention to the family, wishing that his children, and nephew will start legitimate businesses and their children reap the benefits.<br /><br />So far so good but then the story splits into several subplots. One is Cross' business (he is the son of Don's nephew) in vagas, running a casino. Instead of showing how such business, being important piece of mafia's work, is done we get to see almost nothing of it. Some links to organised crime are there, but overall this is mostly a dead end.<br /><br />Another subplot is Hollywood business, behind-the-scenes workings of studio. This story takes most of the book but is pointless and could easily be trimmed down to 10% of its entire size. It has only limited connection to alledged main story, mostly through Cross' sister, who is not involved in mafia business. I kept expecting how worlds will finally meet, collide and how each side's inner workings will be pitted against each other, but that happens only toward the end and briefly.<br /><br />Even "going legit" plan is touched only briefly, Don's sons are there but reduced to supporting characters, popping up from time to time but playing little part in overall progress of the story.<br /><br />Then there is Santadio war. We learn it was this that allowed Clericuzio family to dominate, Don lost his son in it and Cross' father played an important role in it. Beyond that nobody in the family wants to talk about and Cross' questions are repeatedly left unanswered. I understand this was before the internet and Google but are we supposed to believe family was Cross only potential source of information? What prevented him from walking in nearest library and looking through newspaper archives? In the end when entire story is finally revealed it's anticlimatic after all that buil up.<br /><br />In the end one can wonder what did Puzo attempt to write about? This is clearly not a book about mafia family "going legit" because we see little of those attempts. It's not a story about influence of organised crime in Vegas because we see little of that either. It's not a story about influence of organised crime in Hollywood because that subplot has weak links to main protagonists. And whole Santadio war subplot is handled badly, in the end resolution feels rushed and forced, simply mechanical closing of open ends.<br /><br />Bad book and waste of time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17411045268450190413" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_251168844" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/251168844" />
    <a title="Greg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7251996-greg"><img alt="Greg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1377948814p2/7251996.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/251168844?book_show_action=true">Dec 26, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Greg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Greg" href="/user/show/7251996-greg">Greg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7251996-greg?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer251168844" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3543692753539516609">Mario Puzo had some good ideas in this book, and I remember parts of it because they were very entertaining. But I have to agree with what some other readers have said. That it was slow and had too many unnecessary chapters. The characters are not really likeable but they're not meant to be. As someone wrote "It is a story of love, lust, loyalty, and betrayal. It reeks of cynicism and violence. It is about a world without integrity where everyone has a price. And it is written with such pace and</span>
  <span id="freeText3543692753539516609" style="display:none">Mario Puzo had some good ideas in this book, and I remember parts of it because they were very entertaining. But I have to agree with what some other readers have said. That it was slow and had too many unnecessary chapters. The characters are not really likeable but they're not meant to be. As someone wrote "It is a story of love, lust, loyalty, and betrayal. It reeks of cynicism and violence. It is about a world without integrity where everyone has a price. And it is written with such pace and conviction that it is totally believeable."<br /><br />I would rate it three stars out of five but it's definitely not worth five stars. A higher mark would have required fewer pages, less characters, less violence, more loyalty, and more intelligent sayings.<br /><br />The best line of the book comes from Don Domenico Clericuzio when says "It is dangerous to be reasonable with stupid people."<br /><br />He explains this to Cross De Lena when he talks about the Santadio War. He wishes he had not been reasonable with the Santadios. That he should have wiped them out from the start. That he was too patient. This is disturbing to read because it sounds like he is trying to convince the reader to have less patience with people. It is true that all the great Dons excelled in patience but they knew when to stop, Puzo writes.<br /><br />Pippi De Lena (Cross's father) shoots some guy dead at a restaurant which reminds us of that famous movie scene in The Godfather. He is eventually found "not guilty" in court. But his wife is not impressed because she knows he did it.<br /><br />It's a pity Puzo wrote too many pages. He didn't know when to stop.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3543692753539516609" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer181145342" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2564487583108055676">I didn't know what to expect when I started this book but I was pleasantly surprised. Just going by the reviews here on Goodreads it seems that people either really like this book or are really disappointed by it. I think the reason the responses are so polarized is that people are basing this book on the Godfather book and movie which is really unfair. Believe it or not, I have never read The Godfather nor have I seen the movie. That may or may not have played a factor in my appreciation of thi</span>
  <span id="freeText2564487583108055676" style="display:none">I didn't know what to expect when I started this book but I was pleasantly surprised. Just going by the reviews here on Goodreads it seems that people either really like this book or are really disappointed by it. I think the reason the responses are so polarized is that people are basing this book on the Godfather book and movie which is really unfair. Believe it or not, I have never read The Godfather nor have I seen the movie. That may or may not have played a factor in my appreciation of this book. Whatever the case may be this is a good book. It opens the Hollywood world up, it has suspense and even though the characters are less than moral you still find yourself pulling for them. I read one review where the reviewer's complaint was that they had to read almost the entire book to get to exciting stuff at the end. I believe that is called "plot build-up" and the book is good enough to keep your attention until the very last page. I would suggest you reading this book but you may want to keep your eye on that guy on the park bench while you do.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2564487583108055676" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2232300968" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2232300968" />
    <a title="Harold" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1016399-harold"><img alt="Harold" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1282598389p2/1016399.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2232300968?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Harold" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Harold" href="/user/show/1016399-harold">Harold</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2232300968" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3679154960386954757">Heavy shades of “The Godfather” happening here. Many characters are counterparts to their earlier incarnations in “The Godfather” in a further exploration of the pitfalls that can occur in attempting to transition from the rackets to legit business. It could be argued that even the story arc bears some resemblance to "The Godfather."<br/><br/>It's true that the ultimate goal of many of the organized crime figures of the first half of the twentieth century was to go legit, and the high percentage of the so</span>
  <span id="freeText3679154960386954757" style="display:none">Heavy shades of “The Godfather” happening here. Many characters are counterparts to their earlier incarnations in “The Godfather” in a further exploration of the pitfalls that can occur in attempting to transition from the rackets to legit business. It could be argued that even the story arc bears some resemblance to "The Godfather."<br /><br />It's true that the ultimate goal of many of the organized crime figures of the first half of the twentieth century was to go legit, and the high percentage of the sons and grandsons of the “big shots” that became Doctors and Lawyers exemplifies this. They didn't want their family and offspring in that life. Puzo makes that clear in the Godfather and Vito Corleone's aspirations for Michael.<br /><br />In this book (and somewhat in “The Godfather”) you get the sense that Puzo is telling us that these people really thought of themselves as special – as some form of feudal royalty. I think they would rather of thought of themselves as business men. I think these guys were fully aware of what they were, how unsavory it really was, how much they didn't want to be violent, and how much they wanted better for their family. The wives and children were shielded and didn't really know the whole score. <br /><br />The Socioeconomic factors of the U.S. In the first half of the 20th century were not charitable to Jews and Italians. The America of the late 19th and early 20th centuries was very unwelcoming to them. Racketeering was often a viable option. As things got better for one immigrant group and they were eventually assimilated, new immigrant groups came into the country, faced the same discrimination as the earlier groups and the same dynamic occurred. Anyway...enough sociology. Puzo wrote a fine novel and at the end he leaves no loose ends. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3679154960386954757" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<span id="freeTextContainer11738623918295487363">This novel was fairly typical of Mario Puzo's body of work, and let me hasten to add that is not meant in any was to be disparaging. I'm only saying that this book is representative of the particular genre and style that the author is famous for. While his writings don't leave the reader pondering the deeper meanings within a story, such as say a work by Mailer, Puzo did entertain us, and after all, can you ask for more?<br/>As a bonus, aside from depicting the activities of an organized crime family</span>
  <span id="freeText11738623918295487363" style="display:none">This novel was fairly typical of Mario Puzo's body of work, and let me hasten to add that is not meant in any was to be disparaging. I'm only saying that this book is representative of the particular genre and style that the author is famous for. While his writings don't leave the reader pondering the deeper meanings within a story, such as say a work by Mailer, Puzo did entertain us, and after all, can you ask for more?<br />As a bonus, aside from depicting the activities of an organized crime family, Puzo gives us an insider's look into the movie making business - what really goes on in Hollywood and everthing it takes to make a movie. He reveals this from the aspect of all the vairous players, actor, producer, director, screen writer, lawyer, etc. And who would know better? After all, he authored nine screenplays, some of the movies becoming huge hits. Also, he did the screen adaptations for all of the Godfather films.<br />All I'll say is that this was a fun read.<br /><br /><br /> </span>

<div id="review_1132247947" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1132247947" />
    <a title="Weegee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37603049-weegee"><img alt="Weegee" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1132247947?book_show_action=true">Dec 15, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Weegee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Weegee" href="/user/show/37603049-weegee">Weegee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1132247947" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3819400696045239903">Another Puzo classic. Very good read. Although, one must not get caught up with the scenarios from The Godfather book as it may distract you from appreciating the story and you just might fall into comparing the two novels. that was what happened to me at the start. But, as I went along with the book, I started to appreciate its own plot and development, independent from The Godfather. however, there are just some chapters that are too lengthy and parts that are too boring but rest assured, thes</span>
  <span id="freeText3819400696045239903" style="display:none">Another Puzo classic. Very good read. Although, one must not get caught up with the scenarios from The Godfather book as it may distract you from appreciating the story and you just might fall into comparing the two novels. that was what happened to me at the start. But, as I went along with the book, I started to appreciate its own plot and development, independent from The Godfather. however, there are just some chapters that are too lengthy and parts that are too boring but rest assured, these parts are avenged by the whole book.<br /><br />The convergence of Hollywood and Las Vegas may or may not have helped the whole story. I can't decide. On one hand, at some point in the book I remember wishing for the Hollywood part to be over. It sometimes got a little redundant (secret sex, hidden desires, and financial deals). I also feel that the author should have included a little more deaths and violence in it. On the other hand, I loved how the two worlds collided in the whole story. Just like Hollywood and Las Vegas, the worlds of two blood cousins Cross and Dante smashed into each other as the Family's past forced them to make tough decisions. <br /><br />Personally, I feel that the book is full of surprises from the characters. Some of the parts may be "predictable" (after reading Puzo's The Godfather), the characters would surely catch you off guard. I personally had two to three moments where I randomly cursed after a character twist happened. Honestly, this kept me going and since I have read Puzo's Godfather and The Sicilian, I know character twists are in store.<br /><br />Overall, this book is a must-read for all who appreciate the Mafia. Another classic by Mario Puzo--a true Mafioso.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3819400696045239903" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_389362175" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/389362175" />
    <a title="Peter Molnar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9685981-peter-molnar"><img alt="Peter Molnar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1338078688p2/9685981.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/389362175?book_show_action=true">Aug 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter Molnar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter Molnar" href="/user/show/9685981-peter-molnar">Peter Molnar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer389362175" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15868013289701972129">"The Last Don" is Mario Puzo at his best, rich with timeless, meticulously constructed characters and twists and brutal turns that made his landmark novel "The Godfather" the masterwork that it is today. His creation of a more modernized Michael Corleone, Cross Domenica, walks the pages of this novel with all of the confident swagger of Sonny Corleone and the righteous indignation of Michael. The glaring difference here is that the Don of this novel, in comparison with Don Corleone, is not nearl</span>
  <span id="freeText15868013289701972129" style="display:none">"The Last Don" is Mario Puzo at his best, rich with timeless, meticulously constructed characters and twists and brutal turns that made his landmark novel "The Godfather" the masterwork that it is today. His creation of a more modernized Michael Corleone, Cross Domenica, walks the pages of this novel with all of the confident swagger of Sonny Corleone and the righteous indignation of Michael. The glaring difference here is that the Don of this novel, in comparison with Don Corleone, is not nearly as endearing a character but this is because the events of the novel do not specifically revolve around him. He is not as clearly developed as Don Corleone was and if anything, this would have been something I had preferred the writer address. Puzo also invites the reader into the seedy underworld of the movie business and exposes the backalley deals and the broad-daylight swindles that are involved with getting big movies made and small-budget films barely in the can. I know I will never be able to look at movie stars and producers the same way again after having read this masterwork. Puzo is very much at home with this genre he arguaby single-handedly defined, and it was a great pleasure reading him in such a familiar element. I highly recommend this book and mourn its author, who was gone too soon. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15868013289701972129" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

It's interesting to read about the Family, and their actions and what they are trying to do to be legal, while some people would rather just keep on doing crime.</span>

<div id="review_2786052971" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2786052971" />
    <a title="Ramon van Dam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36069918-ramon-van-dam"><img alt="Ramon van Dam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1413643045p2/36069918.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2786052971?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ramon van Dam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ramon van Dam" href="/user/show/36069918-ramon-van-dam">Ramon van Dam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2786052971" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3205560632223306720">Once again Mario Puzo has given us a novel about the mafia that is filled with interesting characters and pageturning plot developments. He truly had a gift for inventing fictional inhabitants of his worlds that feel as real as you and I myself are.<br/><br/>The story is very interesting from start to finish, except for a section around the middle part that felt a bit too much like an excuse to stretch the length of the book.<br/><br/>Even though there are no direct connections, one obviously has to compare this to</span>
  <span id="freeText3205560632223306720" style="display:none">Once again Mario Puzo has given us a novel about the mafia that is filled with interesting characters and pageturning plot developments. He truly had a gift for inventing fictional inhabitants of his worlds that feel as real as you and I myself are.<br /><br />The story is very interesting from start to finish, except for a section around the middle part that felt a bit too much like an excuse to stretch the length of the book.<br /><br />Even though there are no direct connections, one obviously has to compare this to The Godfather. The Last Don pales in comparison, but which novel wouldn't? There are many similarities and Puzo's experience with Hollywood that he put into this one set it apart enough to let it stand on its own feet.<br /><br />I can see how others might feel that the plot contains too much padding and evolves too slowly at times, but I wasn't bothered by it. The fact that I've finished it in a couple of days probably says enough. I was highly entertained, Puzo just has a writing style that I really connect to.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3205560632223306720" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1325352974" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1325352974" />
    <a title="KP" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4651273-kp"><img alt="KP" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1555622291p2/4651273.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1325352974?book_show_action=true">Jan 14, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="KP" class="user" itemprop="url" name="KP" href="/user/show/4651273-kp">KP</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1325352974" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7923573897533938610">A wonderful, wonderful book with a ruined ending. It had none of the charm or charisma that made the Godfather a legend, and you know why? <br/>It was told in Cross’s point of view, and Cross was ultimately subdued. So basically you end up with a hero who ultimately wasn’t powerful enough and by extension, the story wasn’t powerful enough.<br/>Besides, what fun is it to read about a pawn? It’s the master puppeteer you want to read about.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('e8eedFNij22K4DV9yg736gk5lIUfsuPgjTFw+0YVsSPYhM4IThnf7FWWTb1RhFOfRN6yVu5USmwQGsAGdf8VKQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('CEpJM93HEHKZr6XyKMZYrwbLuFS6AXHIbwyo8UnlK7WrCRlPwLxA80bZ3TKzTPzaSyyeh0vn2ETyJxgMeg+Pvw==')})
  }
//]]>

Mark Winegardner brings an original voice and vision to Mario Puzo\'s mythic characters while creating several equally unforgettable characters of his own. In his bloody rise from soldier to mob boss, he encounters ever more barbaric betrayals--in friendship, in his brutal business, in love-- yet simultaneously comes to understand the meaning of loyalty, the virtue of relationships, and gains a perspective on the lonely, if powerful, life he has chosen.As the years pass, as enemies are made and defeated, as wars are fought and won, the old don meets an abandoned boy who needs a parent as much as protection. Setting a new standard for this acclaimed author, Gangster is a compassionate portrait of one man\'s fight against his fate--and an unforgettable epic of a family, a city, a century.From the Hardcover Joje minimi didieji karvedžiai – jų laimėti mūšiai ir klaidos, kainavusios tūkstančius gyvybių. Joje minimi paprasti kareiviai ir jų prisiminimai, kartais svarbesni už prezidentų laiškus. Joje minimi žmonės, kurie išgyveno tai, ko neįmanoma įsivaizduoti net didžiausiame košmare. Mums tai istorija, mums šiais laikais tai atrodo neįmanoma. Bet ar tikrai tai negali pasikartoti dar kartą? Tačiau nieko nėra parašyta lietuvių kalba. In this riveting story, rich with family ties and intrigue, award-winning Boston Globe reporters Kevin Cullen and Shelley Murphy follow Whitey\'s extraordinary criminal career--from teenage thievery to bank robberies to the building of his underworld empire and a string of brutal murders. It was after a nine-year stint in Alcatraz and other prisons that Whitey reunited with his brother William &quot;Billy&quot; Bulger, who was soon to become one of Massachusetts\'s most powerful politicians. He also became reacquainted with John Connolly, who had grown up around the corner from the Bulgers and was now--with Billy\'s help--a rising star at the FBI.\n\nOnce Whitey emerged triumphant from the bloody Boston gang wars, Connolly recruited him as an informant against the Mafia. Their clandestine relationship made Whitey untouchable; the FBI overlooked gambling, drugs, and even homicide to protect their source. Among the close-knit Irish community in South Boston, nothing was more important than honor and loyalty, and nothing was worse than being a rat. Whitey is charged with the deaths of nineteen people killed over turf, for business, and even for being informants; yet to this day he denies he ever gave up his friends or landed anyone in jail.\n\nBased on exclusive access and previously undisclosed documents, Cullen and Murphy explore the truth of the Whitey Bulger story. They reveal for the first time the extent of his two parallel family lives with different women, as well as his lifelong paranoia stemming in part from his experience in the CIA\'s MKULTRA program. They describe his support of the IRA and his hitherto-unknown role in the Boston busing crisis, and they show a keen understanding of his mindset while on the lam and behind bars. The result is the first full portrait of this legendary criminal figure--a gripping story of wiseguys and cops, horrendous government malfeasance, and a sixteen-year manhunt that climaxed in Whitey\'s Though Kuznetsov seems like the perfect candidate to take on the corruption in Russian politics, his reputation becomes clouded when Boris Benz, his business partner and best friend, turns up dead. In a land of shamans and brutally cold nights, oligarchs wealthy on northern oil, and sea monsters that are said to prowl the deepest lake in the world, Renko needs all his wits about him to get Tatiana out alive.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/12605.Mario_Puzo">About Mario Puzo</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/12605.Mario_Puzo"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1379918709p3/12605.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/12605.Mario_Puzo">Mario Puzo
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
3,393
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9159624576958020088">Puzo was born in a poor family of Neapolitan immigrants living in the Hell's Kitchen neighborhood of New York. Many of his books draw heavily on this heritage. After graduating from the City College of New York, he joined the United States Army Air Forces in World War II. Due to his poor eyesight, the military did not let him undertake combat duties but made him a public relations officer statione</span>
  <span id="freeText9159624576958020088" style="display:none">Puzo was born in a poor family of Neapolitan immigrants living in the Hell's Kitchen neighborhood of New York. Many of his books draw heavily on this heritage. After graduating from the City College of New York, he joined the United States Army Air Forces in World War II. Due to his poor eyesight, the military did not let him undertake combat duties but made him a public relations officer stationed in Germany. In 1950, his first short story, The Last Christmas, was published in American Vanguard. After the war, he wrote his first book, The Dark Arena, which was published in 1955.<br /><br />At periods in the 1950s and early 1960s, Puzo worked as a writer/editor for publisher Martin Goodman's Magazine Management Company. Puzo, along with other writers like Bruce Jay Friedman, worked for the company line of men's magazines, pulp titles like Male, True Action, and Swank. Under the pseudonym Mario Cleri, Puzo wrote World War II adventure features for True Action.<br /><br />Puzo's most famous work, The Godfather, was first published in 1969 after he had heard anecdotes about Mafia organizations during his time in pulp journalism. He later said in an interview with Larry King that his principal motivation was to make money. He had already, after all, written two books that had received great reviews, yet had not amounted to much. As a government clerk with five children, he was looking to write something that would appeal to the masses. With a number one bestseller for months on the New York Times Best Seller List, Mario Puzo had found his target audience. The book was later developed into the film The Godfather, directed by Francis Ford Coppola. The movie received 11 Academy Award nominations, winning three, including an Oscar for Puzo for Best Adapted Screenplay. Coppola and Puzo collaborated then to work on sequels to the original film, The Godfather Part II and The Godfather Part III.<br /><br />Puzo wrote the first draft of the script for the 1974 disaster film Earthquake, which he was unable to continue working on due to his commitment to The Godfather Part II. Puzo also co-wrote Richard Donner's Superman and the original draft for Superman II. He also collaborated on the stories for the 1982 film A Time to Die and the 1984 Francis Ford Coppola film The Cotton Club.<br /><br />Puzo never saw the publication of his penultimate book, Omertà, but the manuscript was finished before his death, as was the manuscript for The Family. However, in a review originally published in the San Francisco Chronicle, Jules Siegel, who had worked closely with Puzo at Magazine Management Company, speculated that Omertà may have been completed by "some talentless hack." Siegel also acknowledges the temptation to "rationalize avoiding what is probably the correct analysis -- that [Puzo] wrote it and it is terrible."<br /><br />Puzo died of heart failure on July 2, 1999 at his home in Bay Shore, Long Island, New York. His family now lives in East Islip, New York.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9159624576958020088" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
